5.5          PC2's FY92-1993 financial results show that it was able to generate revenues sufficient to
cover all operating expenses (including depreciation), as well as interest expenses and income taxes.
Energy sales grew by 17%, from 3,118 Gwh in FY92 to 3,646 Gwh in FY93 and revenues from D
1,660 billion to D 2,106 billion, or 27%. Major portions of PC2's operating expenses were for fuel,
48%, and depreciation, 24% of revenues. Operating income in FY93 was 23% higher than in FY92,
as PC2 was able to hold down operating expenses to 90% of total revenues. Because of increasing
sales and improved operating ratio, PC2's net income almost doubled, from D 76 billion in FY92 to D
151 billion inFY93.
5.6         While PC2's financial performance is satisfactory, PC3's finances are a matter of serious
concern. PC3 sold 638 Gwh in FY93, 19% higher than in FY92 and its total revenues increased by
26%  from  D 243 bilY;on to D 306 billion.  However, PC3's revenues were not enough to offset
operating expenses incurred in either year, and the result was operating losses of D 139 billion in FY92
and D 194 billion in FY93. The difference in the operating performances of PC2 and PC3 is largely
due to their different markets. While PC2 sells to the more compact urban subscribers with a large
proportion of industrial consumers at higher tariff levels, PC3 sells predominantly in smaller towns and
rural areas, at lower tariff levels. Also, PC3 has a higher generation expense as it has a large number
of isolated diesel stati ,-. The Government is aware of PC3's unfavorable financial situation and has
taken actions to remedy it, including reducing the price PC3 has to pay for purchased power from PC1
and PC2 (from D 200/kWh to D 5/kWh), reducing the negotiated fuel/diesel price which PC3 pays, and
adjusting the amount of regular and major maintenance depreciation which PC3 may remit and keep,
respectively. While the greatly subsidized transfer price of power from PC1 and PC2 to PC3 has
alleviated PC3's financial situation somewhat, it has had a negative effect on the finances of PC1 and
PC2, representing about D 90 billion of lost revenues to PCI and PC2.

5.7        Table 5.2 demonstrates the serious weakness of PC2 and PC3's financial condition and the
anomalous situation of its capital structure. PC2's equity base in FY92 of D 4,809 billion, consisting
of Government capital of D 4,838 billion and accumulated losses of D 29 billion in FY92, increased to
D 6,011 billion in FY93 (D 5,889 billion of capital and D 122 billion of retained earnings). PC3's
FY92 equity amounted to D 1,169 billion of which D 1,330 billion was capital and D 161 billion was
accumulated losses. In FY93, PC3's equity increased to D 1,875 billion consisting of D 2,230 billion
of capital and D 355 billion of accumulated losses. Because the finances of both PCs were capitalized
solely from Government contributions, the PCs had no long term borrowing in FY92 and had only D
270 billion, in the case of PC2, in FY93. The ratio of debt to equity, the'refore, had been insignificant
in these years. However, the finances show that while both PCs benefit from 100% Government
funding, they have suffered from a lack of funds so that their ability to finance and plan for operating
and capital works, as well as to maintain properly their existing investments, has been severely
constrained. The main reason for this lies in the Government requirement that PCs remit their
depreciation charge. Up to the end of 1988, the PCs were required to remit 50% of their calculated
depreciation to the Government and retain the balance to fund capital works. In 1990, a change was
implemented allowing them to retain full depreciation, however, due to the Government's budgetary
constraints, this was not implemented and in 1992, the PCs were required to remit anywhere from 30-
100% of depreciation charge to the Govermnent. In FY93, PC2 incurred depreciation expenses of
about D 482 billion of which D 324 billion were remitted to the Government, while PC3 remitted its
entire D 132 billion. Furthermore, the Government also required the PCs to continue depreciating and
remitting fully depreciated assets. In effect, the Government has not provided the PCs with any
permanent cash equity, as whatever Government capital was provided was reimbursed each year
through these depreciation charges. Consequently, the PCs' financial performance and liquidity has
suffered with no ability to finance capital investments, sufficient retained earnings to finance future
expansion have not been accumulated, and financial resiliency to withstand unexpected business crises
has been eroded.
Fixed Assets Revaluation and Rate of Return
5.8        The PCs are required to pay to the Government an annual fixed amount, set by the
Ministry of Finance (MOF), for amortization of assets. Major portions of the fixed assets of PC2 and



-46-
PC3 were initially recorded at cost. With the introduction of a market-oriented economic system in
1989, the Government revalued all the operating assets of the PCs (para. 3.18). The fixed assets of all
PCs were restated based on the price index stipulated by the Government in January 1990 and January
1992. PC2's net revalued fixed assets for 1993 amounted to D 4,028 billion and were only slightly
increased from FY92 levels, despite the revaluation. PC3, had net revalued fixed assets in FY92 of D
672 billion, which dropped slightly in FY93 to D 630 billion. PC2 and PC3 recorded substantial
increase in their fixed assets under construction of about 83% and 55%, respectively, in FY93
reflecting their expansion activities. PC2 recorded a 5.4% rate of return on average net fixed assets in
operation in FY93, while PC3 had a negative return. However, due to the high rates of inflation
experienced in Viet Nam over the past four years, the varying methodologies used for revaluing fixed
assets, and distortions in the value of fixed assets, computations of annual rates of return, can be
misleading.
C. Financial Outlook
5.9        To address the vulnerable condition of the PCs and achieve financial recovery, a re-
capitalization of the PCs equity with the aim of rebuilding, fully funding, and strengthening the capital
base, and to focus on improving liquidity without charging distorted tariffs, is necessary. In order to
improve the PCs' financial performance, and help them self-finance a substantial portion of the
investment program, the PCs would need to retain depreciation expenses. In 1994, the Government has
decided to discontinue the requirement of remitting regular depreciation charges starting 1995. This
Government initiative, however, will not completely improve the PCs' capital structure unless infusion
of new Government equity is made.  The financial projections, therefore, assumes that future
investments will be financed through a combination of loans and fresh Government contributions.
Specific operating and financial covenants designed to directly support the objectives of financial
viability and mobilization of resources for development, are being sought under the proposed project.
The covenants provided in paras. 5.11-5.17 are designed to prevent further erosion of the capital base,
ensure that part of the profits would be available to meet future financing requirements, and safeguard
that internally generated funds will be available to meet operating and debt servicing requirements.
5.10       Projections of PC2's and PC3's financial performance for the period 1994-2000, together
with assumptions underlying these projections, are presented in Annexes 18 through 20. A summary of
the financial projections is presented in Table 5.3.
5.11       On the basis of load forecasts approved by the Government for planning the sector's
development, PC2's retail sales are expected to grow on average at 14% from 4,329 Gwh in FY94 to
9,030 Gwh in FY2000. Revenues from the sale of power are projected to increase from D 2,359
billion to D 9,761 billion for the same period. Fuel costs are estimated to increase by 10% in each of
the years: 1996, 1998 and 2000. The financial projections show that with reasonable tariff increases in
line with the Government's intent to raise the cost of electricity to an average of 7 USc/kWh by 1999,
PC2's performance is expected to remain satisfactory, with operating expenses remaining at about 70%
to 80% of operating revenues. The projections also indicate that during the period, PC2 might be able
to finance an average of about 35% of its future investment from internal sources. However, given
certain weaknesses in PC2's financial accounts and the sensitivity of the financial projections to the
Government's pricing of electricity tariffs and the timing of these increases, a lower self financing ratio



-47-
is covenanted. Agreement was reached at negotiations with the Borrower and PC2 that they would take
all actions, including but not limited to adjustment of tariffs, so that PC2 would generate funds from
internal sources equivalent to not less than 25% of the annual average of PC2's capital expenditures
incurred for that financial year, the previous financial year, and the next following financial year.

5.12        PC3's future finances are precarious and the situation is exacerbated by incorporating on
its books the capital and operating costs of the 700 MW Yali Hydropower project whose benefits would
begin to flow in the next century. For the purpose of this analysis, Yali has been removed from the
financial projections. In addition, the following assumptions are made: (a) the price at which PC3
purchases power from PCI and PC2 would remain at the current level of D 5/kWh; and (b) the
Government would: (i) waive payment of the tax on capital in 1995; (ii) levy tax on capital in
progressively increasing amounts during 1996-1998 to achieve zero net income; and (iii) levy the full
tax on capital and on income from  1999 onwards.  With the load forecasts approved by the
Government, PC3's retail sales are expected to increase from 742 Gwh in FY94 to 1,498 Gwh in
FY2000 or an average increase of about 13% p.a. Revenues from the sale of power are projected to
increase from D 352 billion to D 1,411 billion from FY94 to FY2000, while fuel costs are assumed to
increase by 10% in FY96, FY98 and FY2000. The projections show that in FY94 and FY95, revenues
per kWh of D 475 and D 539 will not be enough to offset operating costs per kWh of D 593 and D
579, respectively, due to increasing costs of operating and depreciating a rapidly expanding system and
will result in operating losses during the period. PC3 will experience net operating losses of about D
88 billion in FY94 and D 40 billion in FY95. However, as revenues per kWh are gradually increased
and as expenses per kWh are held to reasonable levels, PC3 will realize operating income from FY96
onwards.  To ensure that PC3 achieves viable operations, it was agreed at negotiations that the
Borrower and PC3 would take all necessary measures including but not limited to adjusting tariffs, so
as to enable PC3, from FY96 and thereafter, to realize operating revenues that would be sufficient to
cover all operating costs including depreciation. This would be on the basis of the aforementioned
assumptions regarding Yali, the cost of purchased power and the levy of tax on capital.
D. Financing Plan
5.13        PC2 and PC3's financing plan is presented in Table 5.3.

5.14       In the past, PC2 and PC3 have not borrowed long term funds since all funds have been
provided through the Government budget. The projections, however, assume that a bigger portion of
future investments will be financed through loans until a satisfactory capitalization of the power sector
is achieved. The assumptions on the future capital structure are: (a) in order to maintain a manageable
level of debt servicing, the existing Government capital will remain as equity and will not be
transformed into debt; (b) beginning 1994, new investments will be financed with one third new cash
Government contributions in the form of equity and two thirds debt.
5.15       Total investments including IDC of PC2 and PC3 for 1994-2000 are projected at D 32,121
billion (US$2.9 billion) and D 9,298 billion (US$845 million), respectively. PC2's investments are
expected to grow considerably at an average rate of about 36% during 1994-2000, while investments of
PC3 are projected to grow by 15% p.a. from FY94-97 and drop sharply by 53% thereafter. PC2 and
PC3 will secure long term financing, net of debt service, for about D 18,593 billion and D 4,678
billion, respectively, of projected 1994-2000 investments. These borrowings represent adequate capital
structures for PC2 and PC3 with long term debts not exceeding 46% and 37% of total capitalization,
respectively, for the projected period. While PC2 is expected to achieve a significant level of self
financing averaging about 35% throughout the projected period, PC3's net internal cash generation
would not be sufficient to cover investments, as its internal cash net of debt service would contribute
only about 7% on average to investments.
5.16       PC2's debt service coverage in the past has been adequate because borrowings have not
been a significant source of financing. The financial projections show that with the restructuring of
PC2's capital base and implementation of adequate tariffs, the debt service coverage would be between
2.8 times to 41.7 times during the projected period. With a view to maintaining debt service at
acceptable levels, agreement was reached at negotiations with the Borrower and PC2 that PC2 would
not incur additional debt unless a reasonable forecast shows that its estimated internally generated cash,
after expenditure for each year, during the term of the debt to be incurred shall be at least 1.5 times its
total estimated debt service requirements in such years, including the debt to be incurred.
5.17       The bulk of PC3's project construction will be carried out in FY94-97 and about 71% of
the capital investment will be made in these years. PC3 will have no further major capital expenditures
beyond 1997 and will not require any cash generation except for meeting debt service and working
capital requirements. PC3's long term loans would require debt service payments of D 995 billion over
the projected period. The financial projections show that PC3's internal cash generation (operating
profit less income tax plus depreciation), before debt service, would be about D 2,133 billion which
would enable PC3 to service its debt from its internal sources without recourse to additional borrowing.
PC3's debt service coverage is estimnated to be negative in PY94 but will increase to 10.2 times in
FY95 and gradually decline to a normal level as debt servicing to finance expenditures commences from
FY96. Agreement was reached at negotiations with the Borrower and PC3 that PC3 would in each year
beginning FY95, achieve a debt service coverage of not less than 1.3 times in respect of its operations.



CHAPTER VI
Project Justification
A. Economic Analysis
6.1        Development of the power sector is crucial for the economic upliftment of Viet Nam.
Implementation of the proposed project would help ensure the country's continued economic growth.
While providing the much needed capital for investment, the project would make a significant
contribution towards improving the efficiency of the power sector, in terms of reducing fuel costs,
reducing losses and improving system operation and control - all proven measures to achieve benefits at
the lowest cost. Other project benefits would be strengthening of the power sector institutions, preparing
them to handle efficiently the requirements of a rapidly growing system, and moving them towards
market-oriented approaches.
Conversion of Ba Ria Open Cycle Turbines to Combined Cycle Operation
6.2        Although the commissioning of the 500 kV North-South link has temporarily eased power
shortages in the South, the rapid demand growth is expected to put renewed pressure on generating
capacity within the next 2-3 years. An assessment of PC2's generation requirements by consultants
shows the need for addition of base load capacity in PC2's system by 1996-1997. Even with the
addition of 57 MW steam capacity at Ba Ria (proposed under the project), the system reserve would be
insufficient to back up the 500 kV link and load shedding can be expected at times when the link fails.
6.3        The availability of offshore gas at Ba Ria from mid 1995 at a price of about US$2.0 per
MMBTU (economic cost about US$1.3 per MMBTU) strengthens the justification for converting Ba Ria
to a base load station. An assessment of the annuitized costs of different options for capacity additions
(conversion of open cycle turbines to combined cycle operation, new open cycle capacity, new
combined cycle capacity - Annex 21) shows that the least-cost alternative is the conversion of three 37.5
MW turbines to combined cycle operation, by adding a 57 MW steam cycle.
6.4        The economic internal rate of return (EIRR) is calculated for the least-cost option
assuming the plant running at an annual capacity factor of 68% for an economic life of 20 years. All
monetary costs and benefits are in 1994 prices. Because of the liberalized nature of foreign exchange in
Viet Nam, no adjustments are made for the shadow exchange rate. A conversion factor of 0.75 is
assumed for expenditure on labor to remove distortions of subsidies and taxes. The incremental sales
(after duly allowing for all losses) represent the benefits of the project; these are valued at tariffs
progressively reaching 7 USc/kWh by 1999 as proposed by the Government. The cost streams consist
of the capital expenditure (foreign - US$62.82 million; local - US$6.26 million) and the annual
operation and maintenance costs of US$1.73 million (2.5% of capital cost). As the steam turbine will
use waste heat, there is no incremental fuel cost. The EIRR is computed at 18.0% (details are at
Annex 21). A 10% increase in capital costs reduces the EIRR to 16.3%. If the tariff is maintained at
the present level of about 5 USc/kWh, the EIRR falls to 14.0%. Finally, a 12% reduction in output
(unlikely, given PC2's needs for energy and its ability to run the plant at the assumed capacity factor)
reduces the EIRR to 15.5%



-52-
6.5        The EIRR thus calculated understates the benefits to the economy, as consumer surplus
(benefit derived by consumers over and above the price for electricity) is not included. Inclusion of
consumer surplus would result in a higher EIRR, largely independent of the tariff; a lower tariff would
simply transfer the benefit from PC2 to the consumers.
Rehabilitation and Expansion of HCMC, Hue and Nhatrang Distribution Networks
6.6        The existing distribution networks in HCMC, Hue and Nhatrang are old, unreliable and
overloaded, and incur high losses (paras. 4.5, 4.7, 4.9). With the forecast rapid increase in demands,
the power companies would need to make large investments in rehabilitating the old network
components, in expanding the networks to new areas and in connecting new consumers. Part of the
reason that sales are expected to increase so rapidly is that there is substantial pent-up demand because
of shortages in generating capacity.  Detailed feasibility studies for upgrading these distribution
networks have been conducted by consultants. These studies consider: (a) the forecast demand growths;
(b) the investment requirements over the next several years for meeting the forecast demands while
upgrading the networks to acceptable levels of safety and reliability; and (c) estimates of progressive
reductions in losses and improvements in reliability (expressed in terms of unserved energy due to
outages). The following methodology is used for evaluating the EIRR for the distribution rehabilitation
and expansion projects.
6.7         The analysis assumes two scenarios: (a) "with project"; and (b) "without project". The
"with project" scenario is based on incurring the full investments (in the distribution systems and
upstream sub-transmission) for meeting the forecast demands and a progressive reduction in losses and
unserved energy. The "without project" scenario assumes that the system would be able to meet the
full forecast demand growth with zero investments but with progressive increase in losses and unserved
energy. The "without project" scenario makes the unrealistic (but overly conservative) assumption that
the existing system would be able to meet the demand growth. Incremental operation and maintenance
(O&M) costs are conservatively assumed to be the same for both the scenarios, although in the "without
project" scenario these would be much higher. The following are the main assumptions:
(a)   all costs are expressed in constant 1994 prices, making no adjustments for shadow
exchange rate and using a conversion factor of 0.75 for labor costs;
(b)  the capital investment is considered over the 1994-2000 time slice (part of which would be
funded by the proposed project);
(c)  the analysis is made over a project economic life of 25 years, with no increases in sales or
losses or improvements in reliability after 2000;
(d)  the average retail sales tariff is assumed to increase from the current level of about 5
USc/kWh to 7 USc/kWh in 1999 as proposed by the Government;
(e)  the LRMC of supply to the subtransmission level is assumed at 5 USc/kWh based on the
Bank's Energy Sector Investment and Policy Review (Report No. 10842-VN);
(f)   unserved energy is very conservatively evaluated at 10 times the tariff level;



-53-
(g)  the technical losses are valued at the LRMC of supply at the subtransmission level (non-
technical losses are not considered in the economic evaluation); and
(h)  the EIRR of the project is the discount rate at which the present value of the cost streams
of the "with project" and "without project" scenarios are equal.
6.8        The details of the economic analysis are presented in Annex 21. To test the robustness of
the EIRR for the base case, sensitivities are undertaken, relating to forecast demand growth, capital and
O&M costs and the valuation of unserved energy. The resulting EIRRs are tabulated below:
EIRR %
Case                                          HCMC        Hue        Nhatranz
Base                                          55.6%       20.3%      29.0%
Capital costs increased by 10%                51.8%       18.0%      26.0%
Demand reduced by 10%                         47.9%       16.1%      23.6%
Unserved energy valued at 5 times the tariff  51.3%       16.7%      23.7%
6.9         The results indicate robust EIRRs for all the distribution projects; the results for HCMC
being much higher than those for Hue and Nhatrang in view of the large power demand in this
metropolis. The EIRRs are likely to be underestimates because of the conservative assumptions made
about the unserved energy (both in terms of its magnitude and its evaluation) and other factors.
Load Dispatch Centers
6.10       This project component envisages the construction of Regional Load Dispatch centers for
PC1, PC2 and PC3, and strengthening of the National Load Dispatch Center. The investments are
vital; these centers will allow the introduction of SCADA and EMS systems to Viet Nam, and allow the
automation of system control and dispatch procedures that are currently performed manually. The
benefits are indeed large, but are difficult to quantify; an attempt is nevertheless made to evaluate an
order of magnitude of returns that could be expected out of the project. Since these centers will be
operated jointly to control an integrated system, it is appropriate to estimate the economic returns
jointly, rather than for each individual control center.
6.11       The benefits that can be expected to arise are: (a) improved system efficiency as a result of
better dispatch of generating plant; (b) reduction in fault clearance times on transmission lines and
consequent reduction in unserved energy due to transmission faults; (c) reduced staff numbers due to
automation of processes currently performed manually; and (d) improved quality of supply due to
reduction in the amount of uninterruptible power supply (UPS) systems and voltage stabilizer equipment
purchased in the country by industry. The PCs will also be able to collect data on the operating
performance of their system far more efficiently than before, which will assist their short and long term
planning efforts; this benefit has not however been quantified. It should be noted that some of these



-54-
benefits (for example improved frequency control) will only be realized if there is disciplined grid
operation and a reasonable margin of capacity over demand.
6.12       The cost streams are the investmnent in the load dispatch centers and the annual O&M
costs, assumed at 2% of the investment. The benefits are calculated assuming: (a) an efficiency gain of
0.5% on energy generation; (b) a reduction of 20% in the volume of unserved energy due to
transmission line faults; and (c) a reduction in the purchase of UPS and voltage stabilizer equipment
equivalent to 0.1% of peak demand by the end of the project. The EIRR is estimated to be 27%;
details of the assumptions and computation are at Annex 21. Decreasing by half the benefits from a
reduction in unserved energy and in the purchase of UPS equipment produces an EIRR of 20.3%.
Energy efficiency savings alone produce an EIRR of 11.0%.
B. Risks
6.13       The project faces the following potential risks: First, delay in meeting the level of demand
growth to maintain the project's economic viability, due to delays in securing counterpart funds;
Second, delays in project implementation due to: (i) teething problems of working with international
financial institutions; and (ii) unfamiliarity with the Bank's procurement guidelines. The risk of demand
not materializing as forecast is low, as the project focuses on areas of high potential growth. The risk
of undue delays in project implementation has been minimized by extensive preparation work,
institutional strengthening measures and substantive consulting assistance in project detailed design and
construction supervision. Agreement was reached at negotiations that the Borrower would take
necessary steps to secure the funds needed for undertaking the project components.



Chapter VII
Agreements Reached and Recommendation
A.  Agreements
7.1         Agreement was reached with the Borrower that it would:
(a)  review with IDA, on an annual basis: (i) its power development program and tariffs; and
(ii) its proposals for adjustments, and thereafter implement adjustments taking into account
IDA's comments (paras. 2.20 and 2.24);
(b)  review with IDA: (i) the findings of the ADB sponsored Tariff study; and (ii) its proposals
for implementation of the study's recommendations, and thereafter implement the
proposals taking into account IDA's comments (para. 2.28);
(c)  review with IDA: (i) the findings of the ADB sponsored Accounting study; and (ii) its
proposals for implementation of the study's recommendations, and thereafter implement
the proposals taking into account IDA's comments (para. 2.28);
(d)   discuss with IDA any organizational changes in the power sector that would have an
impact on the implementation of the project (para. 3.3);
(e)   appoint independent auditors, acceptable to IDA, and make the audit report available to
IDA within nine months after the close of each financial year (para. 3.28);
(f)  review with IDA: (i) the findings of the Demand Side Management study; and (ii) its
proposals for implementation of the study's recommendations, and thereafter implement
the proposals taking into account IDA's comments (para. 4.17);
(g)  review with IDA: (i) the findings of the Power Sector Institutional Strengthening study;
and (ii) its proposals for implementation of the study's recommendations, and thereafter
implement the proposals taking into account IDA's comments (para. 4.19);
(h)   assure availability of adequate funds in addition to the IDA credit for implementing the
project components (para. 6.13).
7.2        Agreement was reached with PC2 and/or PC3 that:
(a)   PC3 would prepare and implement an action plan, satisfactory to IDA, for progressively
reducing its non-technical losses to a level of 3% (para. 3.13);
(b)   PC3 would prepare and implement an action plan, satisfactory to IDA, to reduce its
receivables, so that on or after December 31, 1996, its net receivables (after provision for
bad debts) would not exceed four months of sales revenue (para. 3.17);



-56-
(c)        PC2 and PC3 to introduce interim  changes recommended by IDA in their
mnting systems starting with the financial year 1995 (para. 3.20);
(d)  P1_ and PC3 would appoint independent auditors, acceptable to IDA, and make the audit
report available to IDA within nine months after the close of each financial year (para.
3.28);
(e)   PC2 and PC3 would furnish quarterly reports for monitoring the progress of the project in
terms of physical execution and financial reports and an Implementation Completion
Report (ICR) (para. 4.24).
(f)   PC2 and PC3 would furnish to IDA the Phase-2 RAPs not later than three months prior to
their implementation, undertake implementation of the Phase-2 RAPs only after approval
by IDA and the Borrower and complete RAP activities at any site at least one month prior
to start of construction (para. 4.27);
(g)   PC3 would carry out the RAP for the 220 kV Krongbuk-Nhatrang line and associated
substations as approved by IDA and the Government (para. 4.28);
(h)   PC2 would: (a) design the plant and follow appropriate mitigation measures; (b) set up an
environmental unit and an environmental coordinator as part of the management structure
of the power station, as outlined in the EIA document; and (c) compensate the shrimp
farming community at rates that have been mutually agreed upon (para. 4.29);
(i)  take all measures necessary to enable PC2 to generate funds from internal sources
equivalent to 25 % of its annual average investment for three years (para. 5.11);
(j)  take all measures necessary to enable PC3 to realize satisfactory operating ratios (para.
5.12);
(k)   PC2 would achieve a debt service coverage ratio of not less than 1.5 (para. 5.16); and
(1)   PC3 would achieve a debt service ratio of not less than 1.3 (para. 5.17).
7.3        The finalization of subsidiary loan agreements between the Borrower and PC2 and PC3,
acceptable to IDA, would be a condition of effectiveness of the IDA credit (para. 4.31). The approval
of Phase-2 RAPs by IDA and the Borrower would be a condition for disbursement under the IDA credit
for expenditures incurred against the distribution systems sub-components of the project components
(para. 4.27).
B. Recommendation
7.4        On the basis of the aforementioned agreements, the proposed project is suitable for an IDA
credit of SDR 110.6 million (US$165 million equivalent) to the Socialist Republic of Viet Nam on
IDA's standard terms with a maturity of 40 years.

RESOLUTION No. 1592/OD/TCCB3
ON THE FUNCTIONS. DUTIES AND
THE ORGANIZATION OF THE POWER COMPANY
OF THE SOUTH
(Hanoi, the 7th of August 1976)
The Minister of Power and Coal:
-    Based on the Resolution No. 172-P dated November 1, 1973 of the Cabinet Council establishing
the duties, authority and responsibility of the Minister in the economical management of his
responsibilities;
-    reviewing the need of the unified country with respect to the management needs of the total
power sector in the whole country and the consolidated need of the sector in the south in
particular; and
-    reviewing the suggestions of the Chief of the Personnel Department.
RESOLUTION
Article 1:  The name of the "General Department of Power" is changed to read "Power Company of
the South". The company is a unit placed directly under the Ministry of Power and Coal
operating on a cost accounting basis, in theory covering costs through revenues and
making a profit. The company's headquarter is located in Ho Chi Minh City.
Article 2:  The company has the following responsibilities and statutes:
1.   Provide guidance to the departments in the company to realize the plans for power
generation and transmission, the management and distribution of power, the economics of

the sale of power, construction and development of power facilities, reconstruction of the
power network, guarantee the supply of power for productive purposes and daily usage in
the area under the responsibility of the company from the provinces of Lam Dong and
Thuan Hai to Minh Hai, including Ho Chi Minh City.
2.   The process of development of power in the South is accountable to the Ministry,
including:
establishment of a power development plan in accordance with the directions of the
Ministry;
provide guidance to the Ministry on and manage capital construction (proposal to
the Ministry on strategy of construction, policy of investme.- guidance on

companies, guarantee that investment grows) following the principles, procedures
and management systems of the Government and the Ministry for capital

RESOLUTION No.388-HDBT
(Hanoi, the 20th of November 1991)
Under Resolution No. 388-HDBT - it is a "Temporary Resolution and the intention is, to pass a
regular law to this effect in the future - all State Enterprises, including those dealing with electric
power, had to apply for re-registration. The purpose of this procedure was to review the very large
number of State Enterprises, which had accumulated over the years, with a view to determining whether
they would be economically viable under the regime of an Open Market Policy. Following guidelines
were applied in determining viability:
1.   Companies had to be in a position to operate in the sector they had been operating before.
2.   Prospects had to be good for preserving and maintaining the capital entrusted to them by the
State.
3.   They had to be in a position to carry out the duties and responsibilities entrusted to them by the
State.
4.   Adequate staffing and a suitable organization had to be in place.
5.   The companies had to be in a position to preserve their assets and to satisfy their market.
In the case of the power companies, the Ministry of Energy (MOE) had to submit the application for re-
registration.
The process of re-registration was originally envisaged as follows:
1.   Enterprises with a considerable amount of assets)"
(a)   Review by the Chairman of the Planning Commission.
(b)   Decision by the Prime Minister.
I1 No definision was given on what constitutes a 'Considerable Amount of Assets".



-70-
Annex 4
Page 6 of 7
2.   "Smaller" amount of assets
(a) For centrally-owned companies the Chairman of the Planning Commission was to decide
on re-registration of State Enterprises.
(b)   For provincially-owned companies the Minister of Economics was to decide on re-
registration of State Enterprises.
After approval of the re-registration, a company had to register with the Government ("economic
authority") within 60 days. After this registration the State Enterprise acquired its status of a new
juridical entity. Within 7 days after registration the "economic authority" had to send the necessary
papers to the tax authorities, to the Ministry of Finance and to the Ministry of Statistics.
Within 30 days of registration the State Enterprise had to announce in the official gazette - on five
consecutive days - the particulars of its juridical person, such as name, headquarters, name of bank
connection and account number, date of start of operations, sector of operations, etc.
Subsequently,
RESOLUTION No.393/CT
(Hanoi, the 25th of November 1991)
was published and stipulated the following:
All State Enterprises, for which it has been decided that they be abolished, must discontinue operation
during the first quarter of 1992. Those which remain under new registry must be re-established by the
end of the second quarter 1992. By January 31, 1992 all State Enterprises had to report their intention
to re-register. They have to carry a new seal.
RESOLUTION No. 156/HDBT
ADDENDUM TO RESOLUTION No. 388-HDBT
(Hanoi, the 7th of May 1992)
In modification of Resolution No. 388, it was decreed that, before reviewing a State Enterprise, a
SELECTION COMMITTEE has to be established. Unlike in the basic resolution where the Prime
Minister decides about a State Company's re-registration after recommendation by the Chairman of the
State Planning Commission, the Prime Minister decides, in the case of a comnpany with sizeable amount
of assets, on the recommendation of the Selection Committee (the Committee consists of the State
Planning Conunission and members of the Ministry concerned). In the case of smaller enterprises with



-71-
Annex 4
Page 7 of 7
CENTRAL ownership, a similar selection committee reviews, the Prime Minister decides and
concerned Minister signs the establishment paper. In the case of smaller enterprises with REGIONAL
ownership, the Minister of Economics and Technology reviews and decides. The Chairman of the City
Council signs the establishment paper.
RESOLUTION No. 147-TTG of April 7, 1993 re-established Power Company No. 2 with a Capital of
Dong 3,831,204,000,000.
RESOLUTION No. 148-TTG of April 7, 1993 re-established Power Company No. 3 with a Capital of
Dong 707,607,000,000.
The production and consumption of electricity in the country is regulated with the following directive:
DIRECTIVE No. 80-HDBT
ON SUPPLY AND USE OF POWER
(Hanoi, the 18th of July 1983)
I.    GENERAL
HI.   Conditions of Supply and Use of Power
A.   Regulation
B.   Basic technical conditions, such as cosphi, capacity factor, etc.
C.   Meter reading
D.   Invoicing and Control
m.  Area of Control of Power Facilities. The relevant paragraphs cover the subject of electric power
from generation to consumption.
IV.  Responsibilities and Limitations of Supply. Basically, the power companies are required to offer
safe, uninterrupted supply. In case of major maintenance requirements, customers must be
notified 10 days in advance. In case of emergency, PCs have access to any premise at anytime.
By breach of a supply contract, a monetary compensation of five times the electricity not
provided is foreseen; the same applies to damages caused by voltage surges. Force majeure is,
of course, excluded.
V.   Responsibilities of Users. Resale of electricity is basically not allowed. Repair of power facilities
is exclusively the responsibility of the power companies. Theft of electricity is a criminal
offense.

VIET NAM
Power Sector Rehabilitation and Expansion Project
Power Company No. 2 and No. 3
Billing and Collection System
A. Background
1.           Power Company no. 2 (PC2) and Power Company no. 3 (PC3) have 19 and 11 power
service subsidiaries, respectively, located in different provinces. Depending on its physical size, each
province also has a number of collection branches. PC2 services approximately 700,000 customers out
of a total population of about 28 million in an area covering 22% of the country. PC3 services 158,270
customers out of total population of approximately 11 million in an area covering 33 % of the country.
B. Metering
2.           PC2 and PC3 meter reading procedures are similar. Meter readers, who report to the
head of the power service subsidiary, play an important role in the process of recording the usage of
power. All new meter readers go through a short training course. They need to be meticulous and
must possess legible handwriting.
3.           A meter reader has the following records:
(a)    Meter Card: this card, which is replaced every 4 years in the case of PC2, is attached
to the meter;
(b)    Consunmtion Report: this report, furnished by the computer centers of PC2 and PC3,
provides customer information such as the name and address, type of business, previous
consumption, current consumption, etc. At each power service subsidiary, the report is
assigned to the meter reader on a daily basis at the start of a working day and in
accordance with a schedule of meter reading. The meter reader visits the customers
following the order of information provided on the report. At the end of the day, the
meter reader reports back to the supervisor and submits the following: (i) consumption
report; (ii) letters of grievances from customers; (iii) report on addresses which could
not be found; and (iv) report on notices left at the places of absent customers.
4.           Meter reading is done once every month, usually on the same day of the month. In
addition, the meter reader is responsible for: (a) comparing the current consumption of power against
the previous period; (b) reporting defective meters; and (c) reporting theft of power.
5.           In urban areas, meter readers read an average of 70 meters each day. At the end of the
day, they bring back the consumption reports to the power service subsidiary to have the data entered
by the staff of the computer center. In rural areas, they read about 30 meters each day and bring back
the consumption reports every five or six days to the power service subsidiary.



-73-
Annex 5
Page 2 of 5
C. Billing
Power Company No. 2
6.              The billing system  of PC2, which is computerized  includes: (a) computation of
consumption of electricity; and (b) invoices produced in the month following the consumption period.
7.              The Data Processing Center, located in Ho Chi Minh City, is responsible for this
process which is generated using a Bull mainframe with an older IBM mainframe as a back-up. The 19
power service subsidiaries submit the following information to the Data Processing Center: (a)
consumption diskettes: (data from the consumption reports are entered and stored on diskettes by the
computer staff at the power service subsidiaries); (b) notices on new customers; (c) notices on
customers who have terminated their contracts with PC2; and (d) notices of changes such as customers'
names, new meters.
8.              The power service subsidiaries reviews the data carefully before submitting them to the
Data Processing Center which then coordinates with the Commerce Department who verifies the data
before processing them. Information is submitted in accordance with a pre-arranged schedule between
the Data Processing Center and the power service subsidiaries. For the Ho Chi Minh City power
service subsidiary, the Data Processing Center follows a 24-day cycle of producing invoices for
residential customers and an 8 day-cycle for non-residential customers.
9.              The PC2 invoice has three detachable parts. A sample is illustrated below:
Power Service Subsidiary                  Power Service Subsidiary                      Receipt

D. Collection
Power Company No. 2
12.             The collection system includes the following:
(a)     A  Mobile Collection Unit which is comprised of supervisors and mobile collectors.
Every morning, before going on their route, the mobile collectors are assigned a
number of invoices together with a list of customers. At the end of the day, they report
back to the supervisor the following: (i) the arnount collected from customers or the
bank receipts in the case where the mobile collectors deposit directly their collections
into PC2's account at the bank; (ii) the stubs of invoices (third detachable portion)
already paid by customers; and (iii) the invoices for customers who are absent.



-75-
Annex 5
Page 4 of 5
(b)    A Branch Collection Unit comprised of supervisors, bill controllers, and branch
collectors. If customers are absent, the mobile collectors leave a notice (first detachable
portion) to the customers to pay at the nearest branch. At the end of the day, the
branch collectors remit the collections to the supervisor or deposit them directly into
PC2's bank account. The bill controllers keep track of the list of customers whose
power service may be discontinued due to overdue payments.
(c)    A Bank Collection Unit responsible for collections done through auto-payments,
payment authorizations, and checks.
(d)    A Bill-Cancellation and Follow-Up Unit which follows up on the payment and non-
payment of each invoice and cancels the paid invoices from its books. It coordinates
with the bill controllers and the bill collectors in cases of extended overdue payments.
(e)    A Receivables Unit responsible for controlling the receipts and expenses. It reports the
movements of entries to and from PC2's bank account.
13.           On average, it takes one week between the time the meter reader visits the customer and
the time the mobile collector delivers the electricity bill to the customer. The customer has to pay the
bill within eight days. If the bill is not paid within eight days, the mobile collector leaves another
notice at the customer's place, giving the customer a grace period of one t6 three days to pay his bill
before the service is discontinued. The mobile collector returns for a third time to the customer's place
to collect the bill within that time limit. If he is unsuccessful, the bill controllers will issue an order to
the customers' service to disconnect the service within 24 hours for residential customers and within
three days for non-residential customers.
Power Company No. 3
14.           Mobile collectors deliver the electricity bills to the customers following a routing
schedule. If the customers are not present, the collectors leave behind a notice (first detachable portion
of the bill). The customers have to pay the bills within five days. If the bills are not paid within that
time limit, the collectors deliver a second notice indicating that the power service will be disconnected
if the bills are not paid within three days. The mobile collectors return to collect from the customers a
third time within that time limit. If the collection is unsuccessful, power service will be disconnected
within 24 hours for residential customers and within three to five days for non-residential customers.
15.           At the end of every day, the collections from the power service subsidiaries and from
the collection branches are deposited into the bank accounts of PC3 in the provinces. Deposits from
main collection branches are transferred from bank branches to PC3's main bank account in Da Nang
each day. Deposits from smaller collection branches are transferred only every three days. Unpaid
invoices are usually from state enterprises which have been dissolved and especially from agricultural
provinces experiencing bad harvest. Under these special circumstances, PC3 may continue to provide
power and request the State to recognize the bad debt. For example in February 1994, the State wrote-
off a bad debt amounting to D 300 million carried by PC3 for the agricultural province of Quang Binh.



-76-
Annex 5
Page 5 of 5
E. Methods of payment
16.          The methods of payment to PC2 and PC3 are as follows:
(a)    Cash collected from the customers either at their residences or at the collection
branches. This method is the most popular among residential customers.
(b)    Auto-Payment which is initiated by the customer's bank for the benefit of PC2 and PC3
usually on the same date of the month and upon receipt of the electricity bill. This
method of payment is specified in the contract between the customers and the power
companies. This method is exclusively for corporate customers who have stable
earnings.
(c)    Pavment Authorization where the customer issues a payment authorization to PC2 and
PC3 to collect at the customer's bank. This method is used by most of the companies.
(d)    Checks

VIET NAM
Power Sector Rehabilitation and Expansion Project
Improvement of Financial and Accounting Systems of the
Power Companies in Vietnam
(Terms of Reference for ADB Study)
In the context of its involvement in the power sector of Viet Nam through PCI, the Asian Development
Bank (ADB) plans to finance an accounting/ financial consulting effort aimed at improving the financial
and accounting systems and procedures of the sector. Following are the relevant portions of the draft of
the terms of reference for this effort.
A. Background
1.               The Ministry of Energy (MOE) of the Socialist Republic of Viet Nam is responsible
for the power subsector in Viet Nam. Generation, transmission and distribution of electricity in the
country are carried out by three power companies (PC1, PC2 and PC3) under the direction of MOE.
Under the Government's ongoing program of economic reforms, the power companies are being given
more autonomy in their operations and are expected to be financially and technically independent in the
long run. As a part of this process, MOE now intends to improve and strengthen the accounting,
financial and management information systems of the three power companies with the help of a
technical assistance grant from the Bank. For this purpose, the Bank wishes to engage the services of
a consultant whose terms of reference are given below.
B. Scope of Work and Terms of Reference
2.         The scope of work will consist of reviewing the existing accounting, financial and
management information systems used by the three power companies. The consultant will propose
improvements necessary to bring these systems up to international standards and provide on-the-job
training for company staff. Detailed terms of reference are given below:
3.         The consultant shall:
(i)  review the overall management structure of the power company and recommend suitable
changes and improvements for the efficient discharge of existing and proposed obligations
of the companies;
(ii)  review the existing management information systems, organizational structure and staffing
in the commercial, accounting, financial and audit areas;
(iii) recommend changes and improvements to the current commercial policies and systems and
prepare necessary documentation for the recommended procedures;
(iv)  recommend changes and improvements to the current accounting and financial systems. In
particular, recommend in significant detail, an appropriate commercially-based system of
accounts to be implemented by the power companies;



-78-
Annex 6
Page 2 of 2
(v)  recommend appropriate internal audit policies and procedures for the companies and
prepare the necessary documentation for the recommended procedures;
(vi)  recommend an appropriate management information system for commercial, accounting
and financial functions;
(vii) after approval by the Government, oversee the initial implementation of the recommended
commercial, accounting, financial and internal audit systems and provide on-the-job
training to power company staff; and
(viii) prepare detailed terms of reference and cost estimates of follow-up consulting sern _-s for
further improvements and training in above areas.
C. Reporting Requirements
(i)  Inception Report
4.         The consultant shall submit an Inception Report for Bank approval within six weeks of the
commencement of the assignment. The report shall detail initial findings, proposed methodology and
the timetable for the remainder of the study. Four copies each of the Inception Report shall be
submitted to the Bank and the Government.
(ii)   Draft Final Report
5.         A Draft Final Report shall be submitted for approval of the Bank within nine months of
the commencement of the assignment. Four copies each of this report shall be submitted to the Bank,
the Government and the power company.
(iii)  Final Report
6.         The Final Report shall be submitted within one month of the receipt of comments. Four
copies each of the Final Report shall be submitted to the Bank, the Government and the power
companies.
D. Executing Agency Inputs
8.   The executing agency for the Technical Assistance will be the Ministry of Energy. The executing
agency shall provide free of charge all necessary project information, office accommodation, local
transport, local communications and interpreter services. The consultant is expected to make own
arrangements for secretarial support, word processing and other computer facilities. For that purpose,
personal computers and related software may be purchased under the technical assistance, to be
transferred later to MOE at the discretion of the Bank.



-79-
Annex 7
Page 1 of 2
VIET NAM
Power Sector Rehabilitation and Expansion Project
National Tariff Study
(Terms of Reference for ADB Study)
In the context of its involvement in the power sector of Viet Nam through PCI, the Asian Development
Bank (ADB) plans to finance a tariff study through an appropriate consulting effort. Following are the
relevant portions of the draft terms of reference for this effort.
A. Introduction
1.         The current tariff structure and the tariff levels for the public power supply in Viet Nam
require adjustments to meet the financial and other objectives of the power sector. In order to address
these deficiencies the Government has requested the Bank to provide assistance to carry out a
comprehensive national tariff study. The main objectives of the study are to develop a tariff policy
based on appropriate financial and social objectives and to formulate a suitable tariff structure consistent
with the policy. The Bank intends to engage the services of a firm of consultants for this study.
B. Terms of Reference
2.         The terms of reference will include:
(i)  review the capital expenditure programs and operational budgets of the power sector and
calculate the long-run marginal cost (LRMC) of supply; and develop a computer-based
model for regular updating of LRMC;
(ii)  develop a tariff policy based upon appropriate financial and cost recovery objectives of the
power sector and consumers' ability and willingness to pay;
(iii)  review the adequacy or otherwise of the existing tariff both in terms of level and structure
based on the tariff policy developed in (ii) above and by drawing on, where appropriate,
experience with tariff levels and structure of other power utilities of the region;
(iv)  review the desirability and practicability of having a uniform tariff throughout the country
and the need and justification for special adjustments to cater for the needs of each region;
(v)  review the billing and collection capabilities of the power companies and determine the
extent to which these capabilities may affect the implementation of a revised tariff
structure; and recommend suitable improvements;
(vi)  review the desirability of introducing an automatic fuel adjustment clause in the tariff
structure;
(vii)  formulate and recormmend an appropriate tariff structure which is consistent with the tariff
policy in (ii) above, the billing and collection capabilities of the power conlpanies, and



-80-
Annex 7
Page 2 of 2
will enable the power sector to meet its operational budgets and finance a reasonable
proportion of its capital development program.
C. Reporting Requirements
(i)  Inception Report
3.         The consultant shall submit an Inception Report for Bank approval within six weeks of the
commencement of the assignment. The report shall detail initial findings, proposed methodology and
the timetable for the remainder of the study. Four copies each of the Inception Report shall be
submitted to the Bank and the Government.
(ii) Draft Final Report
4.         A draft final report shall be submitted for approval of the Bank within six months of the
commencement of the assignment. Four copies each of this report shall be submitted to the Bank, the
Govermnent and the power companies.
(iii) Final Report
,.         The Final Report shall be submitted within one month of the receipt of comments. Four
copies each of the Final Report shall be submitted to the Bank, the Government and the power
companies.
D. Man-Mouth Estimate
6.         The proposed technical assistance will require the services of a team of consultants with
expertise in utility management, operations, utility accounting and billing. A total of 15 man-months of
international consulting services is anticipated. The consultants will be recruited in accordance with the
Bank's Guidelines on the Use of Consultants.
E. Excecutng Agency Inputs
7.         The executing agency for the Technical Assistance will be the Ministry of Energy (MOE).
The executing agency shall provide, free of charge, all necessary project information, office
accommodation, local transport, local communications and interpreter services.  The consultant is
expected to make own arrangements for secretarial support, word processing and other computer
facilities. For that purpose, personal computers and related software may be purchased under the
technical assistance, to be transferred later to MOE at the discretion of the Bank.

Annex 8
Page I of 3
VIET NAM
Power Sector Rehabilitation and Expansion Project
Power Sector Reform and Restructuring
Terms of Reference for ESMAP Technical Assistance
A. Background & Project Objectives
1.         The Government of Vietnam is currently developing proposals for a major structural
change in the power sector. On January 1, 1995, the govermnent created The General Company of
Electricity of Vietnam, by decree number 562/Ttg, dated October 10, 1995. The General Company is
an independent juridical entity with the legal name of Electricity of Vietnam (EVN). The legal charter
of EVN laying out the exact scope of its responsibilities and the organizational boundaries under it, is
yet to be defined. Tentative proposals envisage the creation of multiple independent accounting and
legal entities in the core operational areas of generation, transmission and distribution. The Ministry of
Energy has developed a draft charter that is under review and discussion by the Government of
Vietnam.
2.         The objective of this technical assistance activity are to assist the Government in the
following tasks:
(a)   Defining the structural and implementation details for the sector transformation under
EVN. This will involve the clarification of the applicable principles and organizational
options for sector reform and modernization to achieve the Government's objective of
moving the administration of the power sector from central planning mechanism to those
that are more market oriented.
(b)   Preparation of a draft grid code that would allow for the integrated and reliable operation
of the transmission system, and also facilitate increased operational efficiency.
(c)   Define the requirements for the legal and regulatory framework based on the proposed
sector structure that are necessary to separate the distinct roles of asset ownership,
enterprise management and sector regulation. The government currently performs all
three functions without the necessary arms-length separation. While the government
would continue to perform a large part of these functions in the medium-term, the need
for a credible regulatory framework has been recognized. This legal and regulatory
framework would also assist in the paving the ways for future private sector involvement
in the sector.
3.         The technical assistance would be conducted in a participatory, interactive environment by
means of Action Planning Sessions/Workshops under the guidance of a Steering Committee and the
participation of a Working Group of commnitted working-level Vietnamese counterparts from the various
Government Ministries, the State Planning Committee, the Institute of Energy and the three existing
power companies.



-82-
Annex 8
Page 2 of 3
B. Scope of Work
4.         This technical assistance will be provided in an interactive environment that is responsive
to the priority needs of the Vietnamese Government, at a time when major reforms are under
consideration and implementation. A Vietnamese Working Group comprised of about 15 experts from
the different entities in the power sector will be established to work with the ESMAP team on a day-to-
day basis. A series of presentations, discussion and action planning workshops will be used to facilitate
this interaction. A Steering Committee comprised of members from key government ministries an
agencies will oversee the technical assistance.
5.         A study tour to UK, France, Malaysia and China will be organized to allow members of
the Working Group and other Vietnamese officials to evaluate first-hand the institutional structures in
place in these countries, as well as to hold discussions with officials in these countries to gain an
appreciation for the implementation challenges.
6.         In May 1995, a Workshop will be held in Vietnam, where the Working Groups will
present their recommendations to high-level policy makers from the Vietnamese Government. The
Workshop will include international experts who will provide their views on the recommendations made
by the Working Group.
7.         During the interactive sessions with the Working Group the ESMAP team will be prepared
to discuss and provide advice in the following topics:
(a)   objective of reform;
(b)   overview of power sector reform: reasons for reform, reform as a process;
(c)   framework for understanding power sector reform: modes of ownership and control,
vertical integration of activities, competition and monopoly, industry concentration,
international experience;
(d)   structural options for power sector organization: key issues concerning structural reform,
general principles concerning structural reform;
(e)  the process of enterprise reform: commercialization and corporatization of the power
entities, sequencing and transitional arrangements;
(f)   key issues in gauging financial performance of the sector.  Options for diversifying
financing for power sector development;
(g)   key issues in legal and regulatory reform. Relationship of regulatory reform to structural
reform; and
(h)   reforms to facilitate private sector participation in the sector.



-83-
Annex 8
Page 3 of 3
C. Organization of Work & Outputs
8.         This activity will be supervised and coordinated by a "Steering Committee" of high-level
Vietnamese officials from all agencies involved in the power sector reform process, including but not
limited to the Ministry of Energy, Ministry of Finance, the Institute of Energy (MOE), the State
Planning Committee including representation from the Department of Enterprise Reform, the State
Committee on Pricing, and from the Reform Committee under the Government's Office. The Steering
Committee will oversee this technical assistance activity.
9.         A Working Group of about 15 Vietnamese experts will be formed by the Steering
Committee. The Working Group will be responsible for the daily interaction with the ESMAP team.
The Chairman of the Working Group will be responsible to the Steering Committee and keep them
abreast of the progress of work.  For the Working Group, it would be appropriate to have
representation from the three power Companies: PCI, PC2 and PC3; from the Power Investigation and
Design Company No. 1 (PIDCI); the Institute of Energy and from the MOE.
10.        To prepare for the discussions with the ESMAP team the Working Group will collect
material relevant to the reform effort in the power sector and prepare a Background Paper. The
Background Paper will also articulate the Government's reform objectives, and clarify the current
institutional and policy directions. During the extended first interactive session with the Working
Group (3-weeks in February 1995), the ESMAP team will prepare presentations and written material in
topics/areas as required by the Working Group and the ESMAP Task Manager.
1.         The Government will be responsible for the selection of participants to the Study Tour. In
this regard, it is important to select the appropriate participants so that upon return they would be
empowered to act on the experiences and the lessons learned during the Study Tour, The ESMAP team
will assist in making arrangements if deemed necessary for the study tour which best provided for the
substantive needs and interests of the Vietnamese experts.
12.        The Working Group with the assistance of the ESMAP team, will prepare a draft report to
be presented at the final Workshop in May 1995. This interim report will reflect their findings of the
study tour, in addition to the results of discussions with the ESMAP team. After the final Workshop,
the Working Group with the assistance of the ESMAP team will prepare a Final Report that will be
presented to the Steering Committee for further action.
13.        In particular reference to the final Workshop, it is understood that Vietnamese counterparts
are responsible for the coordination of activities in Viet Nam including inter alia venue, organization,
translation, logistics, etc. It is also understood that a strong commitment is required from the Steering
Committee and particularly by the Working Group for an extensive effort throughout this ESMAP
activity and beyond so that the recommendations as embodied in the Final Report can be put into
practice.

VIET NAM
Power Sector Rehabilitation and Expansion Project
Demand Side Management
Terms of Reference for Consulting Study
Background
The Energy/Electricity Sector
1.         Growth in electricity demand has been over 9% p.a. for the last 12 years and this rate is
expected to be sustained over the next decade. This growth has been greatest in the residential sector in
urban areas though it has been high in all sectors. Continued rapid growth is expected as a result of:
(a) rapid industrial growth, particularly in the south; and (b) growth in urban residential and
commercial demand, particularly in Hanoi and Ho Chi Minh City.
2.         Much of industry in Viet Nam does not utilize modem energy use technology and there
are great opportunities for savings through improving energy efficiency. However, the opportunities
for simple retrofitting may be limited in some heavy industry by the needs for major investments in new
production facilities to become internationally competitive. The capital base of Viet Nam industry is
expected to more than double in the next decade so attention is needed to ensure that Viet Nam does not
install technology which has lowest capital cost and unnecessarily high ongoing energy/electricity
demands. Attention is also required to address the rapidly increasing use of electricity in the residential
and commercial sectors. While electricity in the residential sector is largely used for lighting and
cooking, increased use of appliances can be expected with increasing living standards particularly in Ho
Chi Minh City and Hanoi.
3.         There has been very limited application of energy efficiency measures and other demand
side management activities to date, although there is a growing awareness of the need for Demand Side
Management (DSM) in government and utility circles. If the demand for electricity is not controlled,
the requirement for additional investments in the power sector will put an unbearable debt burden on the
Viet Namn economy.
Study Objectives
4.         The main objective of this study is to identify DSM components that could be considered
for financing under an IDA energy sector credit. To this end, and, with particular emphasis on the
south of the country where there are current shortfalls in supply capacity, the immediate objectives
would be to (i) identify a menu of DSM opportunities in the industrial, commercial and residential
sectors; (ii) prioritize and evaluate these DSM sector alternatives, based on their energy sector impact
and cost effectiveness; (iii) define an appropriate institutional and regulatory framework for DSM
program implementation and (iv) introduce a resource planning approach (e.g. IRP) which would permit
the cost-effective assessment of all supply and demand energy resource options.



-104-
Annex 10
Page 2 of 4
Study Scope
Phase I: Identify DSM Opportunities
5.         Data Collection Phase I would begin with a determination of the quality of the existing
data on electricity consumption and electric appliance/equipment ownership patterns and trends by
region (e.g. north and south) in the industrial, residential and commercial sectors. Where the existing
data is inadequate, data collection and analysis surveys/audits would be designed and implemented.
6.         Institutional Capabilitv  An assessment will be carried out of major public and private
sector institutions and supporting entities in the electricity sector that would have a role in implementing
DSM/Energy Conservation Programs. This assessment would include, but not be limited to an analysis
of the organization, staffing and training requirements of the principal concerned entities to design,
implement, monitor and evaluate DSM programs; assessment of educational and technical institutes (if
available) that train architects, engineers, economists, market researchers and the potential to
introduce/strengthen energy conservation/efficiency topics in their curricula; the capability of the local
architectural and engineering community to incorporate energy efficient practices and equipment; a
determination of the local capacity to manufacture components and/or assemble energy efficient
equipment as well as manufacture complete units; the extent of tariffs, duties, import restrictions on
energy development; and the availability of equipment testing facilities in Viet Nam to assist in the
development of equipment standards and building codes.
7.         Technical Potential Assessment  Based on an analysis of the data collected, DSM
opportunities would be identified and assessed according to their technical feasibility. This menu of
DSM options must include all sectors of the economy (with particular emphasis on the industrial sector,
including processes and motors) and will examine actions that will improve the structure and efficiency
of both existing and future loads (e.g. new industrial and commercial development; new appliance
ownership in the residential and small-scale commercial sectors), and load management options that
would alleviate the need for mandatory load curtailment. Based upon generally available energy
efficient equipment and practices that are compatible with the operating characteristics of the Viet Nam
grid, an estimate will be made of the arnount of energy savings (both in terms of GWh and MW) that
could be achieved, under the assumption that there are no constraints on customer acceptance and
program imnplementation.
8.         Identification of Cost-Effective DSM Measures An estimate will be made of that portion
of the DSM technical potential identified in para. 13 above that could be achieved cost effectively. This
analysis will also take into account the existing political and cultural structures as well as regional
differences (north and south). While appropriate tests for measuring the cost-effectiveness of various
DSM measures have not yet been introduced in Viet Nam, it can be assumed that tests such as the Total
Resource Cost Test (TRC), the Utility Test and Customer Test would be used to evaluate cost
effectiveness from a variety of perspectives. This would provide the Govermunent with information to
begin to formulate feasible DSM programs and implementation arrangements.



-105-
Annex 10
Page 3 of 4
Phase Il: Development of DSM Programs
9.         Based on the results of Phase 1, a set of possible DSM programs, consisting of one or
more cost-effective DSM measures, will be identified. Each program will then be evaluated to
determine its cost-effectiveness relative to the avoided long run marginal costs of new supply
investments. Programs will be ranked according to their relative cost-effectiveness and impact on
system demand. The next step will be to select DSM programs and determine the specific issues
involved in successful implementation of these programs in Viet Nam and how these should be
addressed to ensure the programs deliver the expected benefits and then to identify a Preliminary
Implementation Plan which will determine a broad potential timetable and plan for implementation of
the program.
Determine Institutional Needs
10.        Review the institution structures and supports required for the ongoing review and
implementation of DSM. This would include definition of a framework to best: set policies and
coordinate the program at government level; manage and implement the program; support the program
on an ongoing basis; and involve private sector in DSM activities. This component would include
coverage of: organizational structure; staffing levels and qualifications; stiff functions, qualifications
and training needs;  foreign and local technical assistance and training inputs; requirements for
implementing a methodology within the utilities for evaluation of DSM opportunities and integrated
resource planning for generation, transmission and distribution investments; and the requirements for a
review of demand forecasting approaches.
Determine Needs for Regulation
I1.        Evaluate the application of regulations for minimum efficiency codes and standards and
labelling energy intensive packaged residential, commercial and industrial equipment. Determine the
impact that this would have on local manufacture of this equipment and support that could be provided
for local firms to meet these standards. Also examine the needs for electricity tariff modifications to
ensure that economical DSM can be taken up more effectively.
Examine Financial Mechanisms for DSM
12.        Analyze funding mechanisms most suitable for Viet Nam and recommend appropriate
mechanisms for financing the program elements. Recommend allocation of funds to various program
components (such as program design; implementation hardware and operational costs, training, database
development and program evaluation etc)
Establish Monitoring, Evaluation and Database Structure
13.        Determine the most appropriate way for the establishment of an energy use database to
overcome the existing shortage of data. Recommend mechanisms for the monitoring of program
performance for each of the major DSM programs recommended.



-106-
Annex 10
Page 4 of 4
Evaluate Teclmology Transfer and Training Needs
14.        Determine the requirements for training and technology transfer in energy efficient
technologies in Viet Nam for both industry and commerce as well as for potential services providers.
Review the best way that these services can be provided and sustained. Address the application of
information and education programs and the best way of implementing them in Viet Nam.
15.        Determine the impact of technology intervention program on imports, the viability of local
manufacturing and potential for local technology development and manufacture, and assess the benefits
of demonstration projects.
16.        This study should provide a high level of expertise transfer of skills in DSM  policy
development and DSM program design (identification/evaluationlimplementation/monitoring).
DSM Components for IDA Financing
17.        Development of discrete components for financing under the IDA credit.
